 I've got some high-wattage Euro-spec halogen bulbs
 in my 914. Last night the left front low beam went out. 

 It has been my experience that when one of these
 bulbs goes out both filaments go. I think it's 
 really strange that just the low beam on one side
 went out.

 Anyone encountered this and anyone got any suggestions?

 Over the weekend, I re-wired and installed my 
 license plate lights. I took apart the heater
 flapper boxes and wired the rear blower to a
 switch so that I could turn it on and off. 
 Apparently, the contacts had broken off (appeared
 melted) some time in the distant past and the
 previous owner had just taped it up. The switch
 grounds the motor, completing the circuit. 
 I took a little electrical toggle switch and 
 mounted it on the back of the raised speed
 shifter plate on the central tunnel. Soldered
 all the connections and put a tiny hole in the
 carpet so that the head of the toggle switch
 protrudes. It's right next to the defrost 
 lever and seems to work well.

 Next project: installing the console with new
 guages (oil pressure, oil temperature, block
 temperature, clock) and the CD changer in
 a new center armrest.

 Stefan

Subject: electric locks

Before I get into my problems, I want to ask about shop manuals.  This
is my second 944.  The first time around, I bought the official
Porsche 944 shop manuals ($150 at that time).  They seemed o.k. but
the front page said that the 944 manuals were basically suppliments to
the 924 manuals (and implied that I should have those as well).  I did
find the 944 manuals very detailed in some areas and very sparse in
others.  I now have a '89 951 and was wondering if I should get the
new manuals, the new manuals plus the 924's, or some third party's
manuals.  (The old 944 manuals went with the old car.)

My problems: Well, I got into an argument with a no parking sign.  The
passenger door was replaced as a result.  The repair shop finally got
my car back to me last week and the electric door locks don't work
right.  I called the guy up and he would be happy to fix it for me but
since it was a nice weekend, I decided to look into it myself.  I
didn't want to loose the day or two I figured it would take for him to
get it apart and fix it and have no car during the week.

The symptoms are not consistant.  For example, on the drivers side
door lock, a locking action on the key first locks and then unlocks
both doors (the locks pulse down for about 1/2 second).  If you lock
and hold, then you get into a state that you can not unlock the
passenger door from the driver's door.  The inside switch is basically
the same.  On the passenger door, it seems to work correctly however,
when you play with it alot, you discover that the electric locks lock
the door when you turn the key in the direction for the mechanical
linkages to unlock the door.  (FYI, there is a mechanical linkage just
as before so that if the electric locks fail, the mechanical linkage
will still work at let you lock and unlock the door).  The alarm
system is confused as well.  It thinks the car is locked at times when
it is not and vice versa.

For more confusion, there is some sort of state machine which you can
confuse.  By disconnecting the passenger key switch and then locking
the car before reconnecting it, the above symptoms would change.
(They were not right but definately different.)

So I took the door all apart and fiddled around looking and checking.
I had assumed that the repair shop had a few wires crossed.  When I
took it apart though, everything was basically fool proof.  All the
connectors were keyed, etc.  I even went to the troble of assuming the
new key lock was mis-wired and tried the six different combinations of
three wires possible that it could have been wired but I never got to
a point that everything worked all at the same time.

Anyhow, I never did find the problem and finally put the whole thing
back together and will probably take it in tomorrow.  I am curious
though as to how these things work.  The switches in the door handle
are magnetic and I'm wondering why and exactly how do they work.  I
didn't have my ohm meter with me unfortunately.  I'm also curious
about the state machine.  Its this a micro or is it just a bunch or
relays.  (I did check the fuses and also noted that none of the relays
were marked for the alarm system or the electric locks).

Thanks for the help;
pedz

In 7 years, I've never had an H4 bulb (dual filament headlamp) burn out!  I
have lost H1s (fogs), as they seem to go about every 2-3 years the way I use
them.

There's no reason that one filament burning out would affect the other within
a bulb.  The only way this could happen is if while burning out, the bad
filament physically damaged the good one.  This is certainly possible.

        JBS

Something common such as fuse, wiring, or high beam switch has failed.  Did
you *pull* the fuse and check for corrosion?  The old ceramic fuses develop
corrosion and can become nonconductive.  You will most likely have a
component failure, or bad connector.  The wires themselves rarely fail.

I don't know about your relay.  Newer cars are switched directly - there's no
relay.  Are you sure your wiring diagram is for the US model?  Sometimes the
Euro cars have additional lights, and they need the relay.  The US model may
forgo it.


        JBS

I'm no expert in these matters, but...  One piece of quality advice came from Al
Caldwell at last year's Tech Tactics.  He suggested swapping the relays around
as both a method of diagnosis and of getting enough lights operable to drive
home.  If you swap your low & high beam relays and the inoperability swaps,
bingo.

A friend with an early 911 had what turned out to be failed relays.  Same
symptoms as you, except that he had only high beams.  They were of french
manufacture, four of them, with incomprehensible notation on the sides.

Kevin Gross

I'm sure the problem is in the new door lock or sensor wiring.  Did the
repair shop put the passenger side parts in the new door, or driver's side
parts?  Check part numbers.

The central locking is controlled by an electronic box.  It's not relays, and
if the box was damaged during their wiring escapades, it's roughly $100-$200
to replace.  Of course, when you got the car back everything should've been
fixed properly and if it's not, then the repair shop didn't finish the job
they were paid for.

        JBS

Subject: 951 rough cold running

I've had my '87 951 for 3 months now and from the beginning have had very rough
running when the engine is cold - even in late august when cold is not so cold.
Symptoms: until the temp guage comes up out of the white arc (takes several
minutes), the engine heaves and sputters and is otherwise very uncooperative.
This can be very disconcerting when pulling out onto the highway. This only
happens once a day (in the morning). In the afternoon, even after the car has
sat for 8 or 9 hours, I never notice the problem.

I recently had the car in the shop for a pre-driver's ed tech inspection and
asked about this. The mechanic suggested running 3 or 4 tanks of fuel with
Techroline additive. His explanation being that carbon deposits in the
injectors absorb fuel when cold and until they saturate, the engine is running
too lean - the Techroline will supposedly clean this up.

Anyone else had this problem? Does Techroline sound like a potential cure (it
certainly is cheap! only an extra $4 per tank)? A friend with an 85.5 944 says
his has always run rough when cold and has never been able to cure it. He even
asserted that anyone trying to sell you a 944 that says it doesn't have this
problem is probably hiding something even bigger...

From: jims <ICOURIER/FINSERVER/jims%Softbridge@mcimail.com>
To: Doug Fields <fields-doug@cs.yale.edu>
Cc: porschephiles <porschephiles@bcm.tmc.edu>
Subject: Re: 951 headlight question
Message-Id: <14901204160041/0004275051NB1EM@mcimail.com>

Doug, I've replaced both the headlamps and fog/driving lamps in my 951, so
I've got some good advice.  I run 55/100 in the mains, 55 in the fogs, and
100 in the driving lamps.

The headlamps offer the best bang for the buck, and install in about 10
minutes.  I've found 7" Cibies with 55/100 bulbs for around $75 +-.  Be sure
to increase the high beam fuse rating; the stock one will run at the limit
and may blow.  I've measured the voltage drop and it's OK - the stock wiring
is hefty enough to handle the load.  I've had no problems in 7 years.  I've
heard cases of Hella reflectors corroding faster than the Cibies; I don't
know if this is accurate, but my 7 year old lights are still perfect
(retractable lights stay in much better shape).

The Euro fog/driving lamps are a major hassle to install.  The mechanical
mounting is trivial (stock) but the wiring isn't.  I had to run special wires
and connectors and tap into the "central electric" box (fuse block).  There's
an empty relay socket for the driving lights, which I populated and used.
Also, there's a flat headlamp programming jumper module that has to be
changed if you want correct, integrated operation of fog, driving, high, and
low beam lamps.  The Euro schematic shows fogs not being shut off by high
beams, and driving lamps are relay-driven in parallel with the high beams.

The end result of all this is fantastic lighting.  I like the look of the
car's front end with real driving lights, and not dummy panels.  If you have
the money and know your way around medium-current 12V wiring, go ahead and do
everything.  If you'd like to save money and time, and not mess around in the
fuse block, just get the headlamps - they're plenty good, especially in fog
and other tough lighting situations.

        JBS

Pete Gregory, UNIX SA  |  pete@wvus.wciu.edu                  |

************************************************************************

OK,  I probably should have said "adjusted".

Here's what I did:

Remove the dimmer switch, mine is on the left side of the steering column.

Once the switch is out, you can see the contacts that move when you 
switch from low to high.  In my case, the high beam contact was hitting 
too soon.  I soon cured this by bending the contact ("adjusting" ;-)) until
a bump would not be able to jiggle the high beam contact onto the power 
contact.  (I hope this all makes sense to someone other than me!)

Of course, I now had a dimmer switch that would shut off the low beams BEFORE
turning on the high beams.  In addition, sometimes the brights would shut
off from a bump in the road.

At this point I realized that the dimmer switch should be a shorting switch.
A shorting switch first makes the new contact, then breaks the old contact.

Steering wheel off.  Switch removed.  Adjustment.  Adjustment.  Adjustment.

Success!



Pete,

In your last paragraph, the situtation you describe sounds like this could 
be your problem.  

Good Luck.

--Soren            "laforce@krypton.arc.nasa.gov"

Um, I think this could be due to any one of a number of things.  The list
includes:

  - cold start valve, thermo-time switch
  - fuel pump, fuel pressure
  - engine temp sensor
  - air-flow meter
  - fuel injectors: clogged, leaky, o-rings leaking

A failed (collapsed) motor mount will also make an engine "idle rough".

If you're going to diagnose this yourself, get a hold of Probst's "Bosch Fuel
Injection & Engine Management" (pub. Bentley), the necessary tools, and plan to
spend a bunch of time figuring stuff out.  Or go to a Bosch-approved shop. 
Depends on whether you enjoy figuring stuff out yourself, I guess.

I don't know about "Techroline."  Techron is apparently pretty good at keeping
down deposits.  I have heard that it does not clean out existing valve deposits,
just keeps new deposits from accumulating.  What have other folks heard?  A
friend just took apart his '86 951.  He used only good, name brand, premium gas
and found that it has almost nothing on the valves.  He had only used one bottle
of Techron since the car was new.

Good luck, hope this helps, and let me know what happens.  As Patty said, your
car need not idle rough; they don't "all" do it!

Kevin

A number of people jumped all over the tie rod ends for $30.  These
are not parts that will fit on a 911 (or 914) unless you already have
turbo tie rods.  This is just the ball joint at the strut end.
I can get the turbo tie rod update kit, which does bolt onto a 911,
and gives a much stiffer feel to the front (since it replaces a rubber
bushing with a metal linkage), for $120.  I think tweeks sells these
now for $160.

Zfarz I know, the hot setup SHOULD be 7&8x15 Fuchs alloys with
225-50-15s.  Hot shocks would be koni.  Hot bars would be the 951 T-S
bar (26.8 mm) or a weltmeister front (26.8mm ~ $500+ from a dealer).
Rear would be a 22mm adj weltmeister.

Torsion bars would be 26-27mm rears, 200200
front springs from an EARLY 944 (as with torsion bars) these were
stiffer to compliment more flexible A and trailing arms.  Or weltmeister
sells springs too, for about $120.

These are guestimates based on stiffening up the car, and what is
available.  Tires chosen for lowest gearing.  The only 16" WHEELS ON A
944 that came stock were 87-88 928 forged dishes, 7x16" all around.
They only fit 87-88 models. If the car is earlir, and a non stock setup
was desired, anything 7x16-8x16 front would do in 951 or 911 offset,
8x16 or 9x16 rears also available.  Inexpensive 16" rims would be 86
turbo (951) phone dials, usually for about $600 a set at a swap meet.

FYI, 
        Steve

One minor correction to Steve's item.  The 7x16 front, 8x16 rear rims were in
fact an option on '87 (and I assume '88) 944 and 944S cars.  This per PCNA's
1987 Porsche Fact Book.  It also lists 8x16 front and 9x16 rear as an available
option on the 951 (aka 944 turbo).

The front 7x16 forged alloys are "928 style", but are in fact not 928 parts. 
(For that matter, 924's and 911's have worn this style of rim at various times!)
They have a 944-series part number, and are 53 mm offset.  The rears do indeed
carry a 928 part number, and I think I measured their offset to be about 65 mm.

One other note.  Porsche Motorsports apparently now has available a 30 mm. front
sway bar for the 944 series, and a rear bar to go with it.  I forget the
latter's dimensions, and I don't know whether the front bar is solid or tubular.

Kevin

Subject: 911 engine rebuilding and mods

I have a few suggestions and also questions about engine rebuilding and
modifications for my 911. (86 Carrera).(My engine is currently apart.)

I have converted to twin plug, heads were machined; ported and polished
by Olies. I am very pleased with their work and can highly recommend them.

In pressing in new valve guides I cracked two heads at the intake valve
bosses. This is a very common problem, especially with ported heads, and
I could have possibly avoided the cracking by buying special intake
valve guides from Automotion, which are machined to a non factory spec
to avoid this problem. What to do once the heads are cracked?
Bruce Anderson gave me a phone number and address of a specialist in
fixing cracked heads. His name: Michael Stimpson. (Ask me for his number
and adress if you need it.) He grinds away at the cracks, removes all
studs from the head, heats the head to a very high temperature in a
specially controlled furnace, and then fills the cracks with a weld like
special process. He then machines the guide bores oversize, installs
oversize valve guides and grinds everything back to the ported and posished
shape. He did a superb job on my heads, you cannot see where they are
fixed unless you knew beforehand , where work was done and look extremely close.

Now, after sharing this experience I have a couple of questions:

1) I understand a couple of companies like Andial and Porschasport
do a modification to the Motronic intake runners by cutting them open,
porting and polishing them, and then welding them together again.
I am considering this modification. Does anyone know more about it?
Cost, possible improvement (especially for my engine with already ported
and polished heads). Further I wonder, they may port and polish them, but
don't you get a weld burr where they reweld them killing the advantages
of the previous polishing?


2) What is the scoope on Porsche's new torque specs. For all modern 911
engines, (I guess the ones without head gaskets, precision machined head
and cylinder top surfaces),84 on, they changed the torque values to angular
torque. The new procedure for torquing the head nuts is 15 Nm initial
torque and then 1x90 +- 2 degree angular torque. They also changed the
conrod specs to 20 Nm plus 90 +- 2 degree angular torque which I used as
the angular torque is more accurate as it depends less on binding and lubrication of the nut and bolt or stud. My confusion is the following
for the head nuts: Dilavar stuts should be torqued to a different spec
than steel studs. All Carrera engines for the U.S. market however only
have dilavar studs for the lower exhaust side studs, and not for the upper
twelve intake studs. How come there is only one torque value for all.
Also, how come they still specify the old torque sequence for 84 to 87
turbos? Do they still use the old type head gaskets? These cars are
equipped with 24 dilavar studs and torquing is done to 10, then to 32 Nm.
On my engine I have replaced all non dilavar studs to dilavar, and I am
just planning to use the new torque spec for torque angle. I hope
this is the way to go - let me know if you know otherwise.

Karl at Rensselaer, Troy, NY.

Message-Id: <9012071736.AA01817@sirius.cs.rpi.edu>
To: porschephiles@bcm.tmc.edu
Subject: new/used parts


As I explained before, a friend and I have gotten in with a parts
distributor who is giving us dealer/shop prices.  Since we get a
quantity discount, and look more legitimate if we keep ordering parts,
my friend has volunteered to do a little legwork and we can open this
up to the porschephiles.  Before I go into how this will work, I want
to make sure everyone understands why:

All parts places have two kinds of customers: shops/dealers and
others.  We all normally fall into the "others" category.
Shops/dealers get MUCH better prices, and there are two reasons for
this: volume and knowledge.  The former is obvious, but the latter is
perhaps the most important, since it reduces overhead.  If you can
assume the person ordering the parts knows what he needs (or can keep
the part around until someone needs it if he doesn't), you don't need
a return policy (that reduces overhead).  Also, you can set up special
ways to order that are quick, since a knowledgeable person can give
you a part number, and you don't have to waste time talking.
It took us some time to convince this guy that we
met the volume and knowledge criteria.  He hates dealing with "whining
customers" which is why our particular distributor only sells to shops
and dealers (and us).  The key here to people who would like to order
parts through me is that there is NO RETURN POLICY unless the part has
been damaged in shipping.  Also, the universal law for purchasing
automotive electrical components is that there is no returns no matter
what.  That's true of any place, though.

The reason we are extending this to the porschephiles group is that
the extra volume should make us look more worthwile to our
distributor, and the more stuff we order, the cheaper our prices are.
He has year-to-date volume discounts that kick in at various monetary
intervals.  This whole setup is strictly legal, my friend
and I are are amateur hobbyists who are extending a special deal we are
getting to some friends and fellow hobbyists.  Therefore, everything
must be understood to be at a friendly level, there is no business
here.  Of course our distributor doesn't know about this email thing,
I'm not sure what he would think, but my friend (whose name is John,
BTW) deals with him more than me and doesn't want him to know about it
just yet.


With that in mind, here's how I'd like to work it:

1) We will try to make an order once a month, for now let's say the
third friday of each month.  If you need a part or parts, send me
mail, and I will get you a price.  If I get an email msg by the third
Friday saying you want the part (i won't take the initial inquiry as
an order), I will collect these requests and my friend will call them
in.  Be sure to include your shipping address with your request.

2) We will ship the stuff to you COD.  If you want to avoid COD
charges and you trust me, you can send me a check.  Let me know if you
are sending a check so I don't go shipping something off.  I will hold
checks or parts until the other arrives (ie I won't cash a check until
I get the parts, and I wont send parts until I get the check).

3) If you need to return something (remember, only items damaged in
shipping can be returned), send it back to me, and we'll send
it back to the distributor (see point 8 for more).

4) We're going to tack something like 5 bucks onto the price of the
bigger parts, just to pay for what little overhead expense we would
be paying.

5) In theory the more parts we order the cheaper our prices will be.

6) Of course, this isn't a business, so don't expect great service or
any other benefits.  Also, since I'm pretty busy, there may be periods
when we can't do this, and I'll have to tell you to either wait or go
elsewhere. 

7) These prices should be better than anything you can get from the
mail order places, but occasionally they have sales and since they buy
far more parts than us sometimes their sale prices will be better, so
I do encourage you to shop around (I'd also appreciate knowing when
there's a better price, so we can complain to our distributor - he has
on a few occasions beaten sale prices when we've pointed them out).

8) Let me go back to the return policy.  We can only return
things that are defective, so you can only return things that are
defective.  If you order a part you don't need or doesn't fit on your
car, you're stuck.  For 911 and 914 owners we will try to help you
avoid this problem through our own knowledge, BUT 944 AND 928 OWNERS
WILL BE ON YOUR OWN.  Please be honest with me about your level of
knowledge, if you don't understand something then say so, otherwise I
may make assumptions about your knowledge that will cause you to
purchase a part you can't use.  An example would be the turbo tie rod
end case from my first posting about this.  A couple people said they
wanted them, and it never occurred to me that these people didn't want
a turbo tie rod end until one message came in that asked if these
could bolt on a non-turbo 911.  In fact, I think it would be best if
914 and 911 owners include the year and model of their car in every
order, I think this will help a lot in avoiding unwanted orders.  
But always keep in mind, WE CAN'T RETURN THINGS SO YOU CAN'T EITHER.
if you make a mistake even with our help you're stuck with the part.
(no big deal, try to sell it).  

9) If you plan to order through me even after reading all this, then
please keep this message around for reference.

10) My address in case people need it is:

1721 Tibbits Ave
Troy, NY 12180

My daytime phone number is 518-276-2816

I recently asked if anyone knew where to locate the new 356 Performance
Handbook.  I found the answer myself, and thought I would share it with
you:  

	Toad Hall Motorbooks, 1235 Pierce St., Lakewood, CO, 
	phone 303-237-0911

They claim to have every Porsche book in print, and some out of print.  I
ordered the 356 Performance Handbook ($17.95) and a copy of the first
edition of Porsche: a Restorer's Guide to Authenticity ($10 - the usual
cost is about $20, but the second edition has just been printed, so they
are closing out the old stock; the second edition costs $45).

Well, I was going to mention it.  Then I figured I'd already put everyone to
sleep.  But since you ask...  Tech Bulletin Group 4, No. 8912 describes the
change from a 18 mm to a 16 mm bar.  And I quote:

"Models affected: 944 Turbo Model 89 (K)

Concern: To further optimize chassis balance, the rear stabilizer bar diameter
has been changed from 18 mm to 16 mm.

This change occurred from the following VIN: 95 KN 150237

Parts Information: 16 mm diameter stabilizer  PN 477 511 411
                   16 mm stabilizer mount     PN 477 411 313 A

When the existing stocks of the 18 mm diameter bar are used up, only the 16 mm
bar will be available for repair replacement."

Steve asked how to get ahold of Porsche Motorsports.  Two ways I know of.  One
is to call them (011 49 ...).  This is, at least, what Jurgen Barthes encourged
people to do at last year's Tech Tactics.

An easier solution is to call up Andial's parts department and ask them.  They
know the drill, and will want to make some money on the deal, of course. 
Another advantage they have claimed to me is that they can batch orders and
thereby spread out what they state are fixed costs of dealing with the US
Customs folks (and shippers?).

There is no Porsche Motorsports catalog.  You have to know what you want and ask
for it.  To that end, I guess you have to read and ask around.  Andial knows of
some parts that are available and can certainly ask others.  You might try
speaking with factory-sponsored/-assisted teams, like anyone up in Canada who's
been near the Rothmans Cup.  You might get to them through the PCA Regions
there.

There's also Dave Finch who runs his mutant 944S[2] in SCCA.  And let's not
forget asking Bruce Anderson!

Kevin Gross

Subject: leaky 914 master cylinder (?)
To: porschephiles@bcm.tmc.edu
Date: Sat, 8 Dec 90 16:46:08 PST
X-Mailer: ELM [version 2.3 PL8]

The Patient:  1974 914 2.0l

The Symptom:  Persistent loss of brake fluid, getting progressively
worse.  Before I bled them, they often required two jabs to build
pressure, but after bleeding, that problem went away.

I removed the particle-board flooring under the pedals and discovered a
significant quantity of fluid and a lot of ruined paint - obviously a
problem that has been going on for quite some time.  I removed the
"underfloor protection" (metal plate protecting the steering box and the
master cylinder from things you run over) and discovered no fluid on the
body of the master cylinder.  I presume that means that the hydraulic
line joints don't leak (they looked dry) and that the worn seals inside
the cylinder cause fluid to ooze out of the place where the pedal push-
rod thing enters the cylinder.

Questions:  
     Are these the symptoms of a bad master cylinder?  I assume they are,
so this week, I'll order a new one from Automotion and install it next
weekend (unless I hear differently from y'all).  
     Is there something else that may be leaking under the brake pedal
which I should investigate?  The metal lines look fine.
     What are the ancillary changes I should make "while I'm at it" ? 
Should I use a 19mm cylinder (I assume it just gives more hydraulic
leverage)?  What bushings/rubber lines should I replace (specifically,
what should I order when I order the new cylinder - I don't want to have
to place two orders)?
     Approximately how much does a 'better' proportioning valve cost?  I
suppose I should do that at the same time...
     What should I expect to pay for a new 'underfloor protection'?  The
old one's kind of rusty and banged up.  Maybe I'll just bang it out and
sand and paint it... $$$


Thanks,
   -Robert Merithew
    Dept. of Physics
    Stanford University

p.s. Yes, I stopped driving the car as soon as I noticed the leak.  I
     realize that it's pretty dangerous...
p.p.s.  Did anyone order the 914 wristwatch from the 914 Owners Association?
        I lost my watch the day after I received the newsletter offering it.
        I think my subconscious is playing games with me.

Use the 911 19mm master cylinder - it can be fitted with no modifications,
giving a higher, firmer pedal.  This is part # 911.355.012.02.

You can get it from Tweeks for $119.50, or Performance for $129.50.
Just about the same price as the stock cylinder - mine never gave me any
trouble when I had a 914 so I never had the chance to update it.

- Jeff Shorey

Subject: Autothority 944 clutch

This inquiry is for a local PCA friend.  He has an '84 944 which he takes to the
track fairly frequently.  Some time ago, he upgraded his clutch with a
"performance" kit from Autothority, those fine folks best known for their DME
chips.  It included the usual components as well as a lightened flywheel.

Things were fine for many thousands of miles.  Then trouble struck.  Details are
a bit vague (this was at our Region's Christmas party, and I arrived well into
the cocktail portion).  Apparently the kit did not really fit his car properly
and led to bending the splines on the car's driveshaft.  He's out some serious
money and is kind of unhappy.  The parts, I am told, are of some 911 extraction,
although that seems neither here nor there per se.

What he would like to know is whether anyone else has installed this kit, and
what their experience has been.

Kevin

Robert -

If it's the seal(s) which has failed, you can rebuild the cylinder.  A friend
did his 944 Turbo's a few weeks back.  Once he had the thing out, it was
suprisingly easy.  (Getting it out proved to be a major headache, though.  The
turbo's heat shield was the problem.)  He bought the rebuild kit from a local
dealership.

Bruce Anderson has for many years recommended a different proportioning valve
for the 914's.  I believe it is called a Kelsey-Hayes, plus or minus my
spelling.  Garretsons sells such a valve, although I don't know whether it is
the same part.

If you look through the reprints in PCA's UpFixin series, I'm sure you'll find
plenty of Bruce's (and others) advice on the valve and the pros and cons of
going to a larger master cylinder.

Kevin Gross

From porschephiles-request@bcm.tmc.edu  Sun Dec  9 17:51:57 1990

From: "Kevin C. Gross" <0004056890@mcimail.com>
To: porschephiles <porschephiles@bcm.tmc.edu>
Subject: News from Porsche AG
Message-Id: <32901209230423/0004056890NB3EM@mcimail.com>

I heard a few tidbits yesterday, their origin being from within the factory. 
This was, again, at our Region's Christmas knees-up.  I tried to scribble down
the details as best I could, between dodging breadrolls and other foolishness.

As reported earlier, 944 production will cease in the next week (if it hasn't
already).  Its replacement is the 968, which will appear in the showrooms a lot
sooner than expected: sometime in '91 as a '92 year model.  It currently has a
tweaked 944S2 engine in it, and has been turbo-charged.  And here's the problem:
it's faster than the new 911 Turbo!  Porsche, like any company, cannot sell an
entry-level product which performs better than their top-of-the-line.  So it may
never make it to market with the turbo.

The transmission is a six-speed, and the TipTronic will also be available.

On the 911 front, there will be some trimming of the product line.  Porsche once
again feels it has too many competing models.  The targa is apparently slated to
go.

And there is some real excitement in the 928 line.  A twin turbo 928GT has been
DOT certified.  It puts out 450 hp, and has a 3.5 second 0-60 time!  Yes, that
3.5 seconds 0-60.  Come '95, the engine will be upgraded to a V12.

Is it too late to write Santa?

Kevin

Posted-Date: Mon, 10 Dec 90 11:17:56 CST
Return-Path: <pedz@yow>
Date: Mon, 10 Dec 90 11:17:56 CST
From: halley!yow!pedz@cs.utexas.edu (Perry Smith)
To: tynor%prism.gatech.edu@cs.utexas.edu
In-Reply-To: Steve Tynor's message of Tue, 4 Dec 90 10:06:38 -0500 <9012041506.AA16575@prism.gatech.edu>
Subject: 951 rough cold running

Go ahead and post this if you feel it is appropriate.

I had a 85.5 944 and start to have rough running, etc.  I can't
remember the exact symptoms.  I do remember that I took it in for a
tune up twice.  Once by the Porsche dealership and once by an
independent.  Both times, I noticed no difference.  Texaco then came
out with this hype about 5 tankfulls and you will be happy.  I took
them up on the offer.  I THINK I did notice a difference.  I do know
that eventually my concerns with rough running, etc went away.  Of
couse this was through the year and the seasonal change might have had
something to do with it.  My problems were subtle to begin with and
perhaps I just lost the ability to notice them.  I can't swear to
anything but I do have a reasonable good feeling that the car's
performance improved.

The Techroline I think is the stuff by Chevron.  If its not, then get
the stuff by Chevron.  It is suppose to be the best of the
addititives.  I would also switch to Texaco or Chevron gas since it
already has the stuff in it.  I would definately switch to a "good"
gas if you are using random cheap stuff now.

Also, a friend of a friend of a friend..... is a mechanic that works
in the Mister Goodwench sort of place.  He says that they offer a
service where they hook directly up to the gas line a container of
pure Techroline and run it through.  I don't know the amount they run
through.  I would guess a quart or so.  You can get about the same
effect as this by adding the stuff to an empty gas tank and running it
through.  This is exactly opposite of what the bottle tells you to do.

My brother has done this (and still does it on a regular basis).  I
can't say that I recommend it.  I would be surprised if any ill effect
would come by doing this once given your rather severe situation.

BTW, I would say that my old car did not run rough when cold.  I might
not be extremely quick but it was not rough.  My new car ('89 951)
does run rough when cold.  I just got it and don't know what to expect
from it right now.

pedz

From porschephiles-request@bcm.tmc.edu  Mon Dec 10 12:49:50 1990
Received: by WILKINS.IAIMS.BCM.TMC.EDU (AA20907); Mon, 10 Dec 90 12:49:51 CST

From: bart@research.att.com
Date: Mon, 10 Dec 90 13:33:25 EST

can't really explain all this without starting with tires.  tires
are non-linear.  that is, their friction coefficients depend on
the vertical load applied.  so a tire that pulls .9g on a 951
might be good for 1.2g on a car 1/2 the weight.

when the car rolls in a turn, it rolls one amount.  however,
the *weight* transferred to the outside tires depends on
the lateral force *and* the roll stiffnesses at each end.
you're going to get some total amount of weight transferred,
but the distribution will depend on the roll stiffnesses, eg
on the springs and anti-roll bars.

so, suppose you stiffen up one end of the car.  for a given
amount of roll, more weight will be transferred to the outside
tire on that end than before.  the non-linearity of the tires
means that this end will have less stick than before.  bingo!
you've just affected over/under steer.

in its wisdom porsche apparently decided that 951S's didn't
have enough understeer.  (recall that the original '86 951
had 24mm front and 18mm rear bars, and the '88 951S came with
*27mm* from and 18mm rear bars - can you say "understeer"
anyway?)  so they put in a smaller rear bar, making that end
stickier, and voila you have a car that REALLY chews up front
tires on the track.

haven't taken my car to the track since replacing it with the
20mm bar, but it feels better already.  (20/16)^4 -> 2.3 times
increase in rear roll stiffness (the contribution from the bar,
anyway).

Subject: re: 951 rough cold running
Message-Id: <73901211023037/0004056890NB3EM@mcimail.com>

Do not, as was suggested, run pure (or close to it) Techron through your car's
engine, at least with the engine running.

There is a Lubro Moly product for this sort of thing.  It is called Lubro Moly
Fuel Injection Purge.  Performance Products carries the line, and it will cost
you $12.95 for a 10 oz. can of the stuff, plus $35.00 for "an adaptor hose with
instructions."  The PP catalog describes the set-up to be for "mechanics and
experienced do-it-yourselfers," which is probably as close to a caveat as you're
likely to get!

I don't know whether this stuff is any good.  It is almost certainly no better
than walnut-shell blasting the valves, especially for the environment.  Then
again, it is easier to do.

VW/Porsche Magazine wrote up the procedure earlier this year, and if memory
serves, they recommended using two cans.  I think the procedure involves
cranking the engine without sparking.  You don't want to burn this stuff (or
Techron).

As I said earlier, if your car is late model and you have run good quality, name
brand gas bought at reasonable volume service stations, your rough idle is
unlikely to be due to valve deposits.

Kevin

called Andial and talked to one of the chaps over there who asked

Subject: Master cylinders (928)


Speaking of master cylinders, I had the master cylinder on my 928 go on
me.  Milo and I spent a Saturday replacing the original with an Ate
(well, Milo did most of the work, and I did most of the helping and
watching -- thanks, Milo! ;-) and discovered that a goodly amount of
brake fluid had made it into the booster.  The 928 booster is just
different enough from a 944/951 in the way that it is installed that we
couldn't figure out how to get it out without pulling the cam housing
and/or a body panel, so we drained the booster (I'd guess at least 12
ounces of fluid) and installed the new master cylinder.  This leads to
two questions and a comment.

Should I worry about replacing the booster?  The brakes feel fine.
There is a noticeable difference between mashing on the brake pedal
with the car off and with the car running, meaning that the booster is
doing something.  In fact, the feel is about like I remember it before
I had the problem.

If I were to try to replace the booster, how does one remove a 928
brake booster?

I have already ordered the Chilton's 924/928 manual (there is no Haynes
manual for the 928 :-( and the factory manuals are just too expensive
for me at the moment) and will eventually spring for the factory
manuals and updates later.

Perspiring minds want to know.

Cheers,
	Bert

I have Goodyear Eagle ZR xxx tires.  This is not the same as the GT+4
but I presume it is a step up.  I am not happy with them.  I have
245/45's on the back and they are too easy to break loose. (My car is
a '89 951).  It might be that I'm just not use to the extra power.
But there are times on dry streets where the rear tires break loose in
second gear without popping the clutch or anything (on a straight).
In the rain, they seem very good however.  I have had only a few days
of rainy weather since I got my new car though.

On my old car (85.5 944) I first had Dunlop D60 (I think -- the VR
tires, not the ZR tires).  I was pleased with them but these were my
first tires on my first sports car so I wouldn't call myself an expert
at the time.  I used them up after about 20K as I recall.

I later switched to Pirelli P6's and noticed a definately decline in
the car's abilities.  I can't say I was supprised since these were HR
tires.  I had these for at least 40K and was just about to replace
them.  (Tells you how hard they were.)

I have two friends with Eagle Talons (or equivilent).  One who I think
is more knowledgable does not like the GT+4's (which come with the
car) and has already changed the fronts to Dunlops and is very pleased
with them.  The other is pleased with the GT+4's but this is, again,
his first sports car and probably doesn't know how good good can be.

I hope this helps and I also hope that others reply to your message
and to this message as well.  As I mentioned at the top, I'm not
please with what I have and I'm curious if I'm expecting too much or
if the Eagles are not a good tire after all.

pedz

This is not the first time I've heard complaints about Eagles
regarding getting the power down. 

> I later switched to Pirelli P6's and noticed a definately decline in
> the car's abilities.

I have no problem concurring from personal experience. IMHO P6's
really suck. The breakaway is sudden and completely uncontrollable,
leading to nasty encounters with the guardrail if you take things
to the limit (not that I ever met said guardrail in person, but
someone else did while driving my car). I got rid of them as soon
as I could, switching to Yokohama A001R (at the time they didn't
have the A008R's) and was richly rewarded: they stuck like magnets
stick to the refrigerator, and breakaway is smooth and clean.
Since then I've changed to A008R's, which are even stickier, but
aren't quite as good in the rain.

> I can't say I was supprised since these were HR
> tires.  I had these for at least 40K and was just about to replace
> them.  (Tells you how hard they were.)

The HR rating has very little to do with performance characteristics,
other than high-speed rating: don't exceed 120mph for more than brief periods.
Big deal. The tires just suck: no traction, poor transient characteristics,
and they wore out pretty quickly too (for me). The Yoko's I'm running now
are H rated, and they are the best I've come across.

> I have two friends with Eagle Talons (or equivilent).  One who I think
> is more knowledgable does not like the GT+4's (which come with the
> car) and has already changed the fronts to Dunlops and is very pleased

Fun looking little car. I'd like to try it sometime.

> please with what I have and I'm curious if I'm expecting too much or
> if the Eagles are not a good tire after all.

Eagles seem OK, but not great (hearsay only, I've never used them).
For real fun, though, get some really sticky tires (Yoko or BFG "R" compound)
and try them for a while.

Gee, sorry to be the odd man out.  I have my car's original P6's, which I wear
in the off-season and any foul weather.  I have been quite happy with them, and
found them to be pretty predictable under duress.  Of course, I don't push the
car very hard with them; that I save for the summer-fun Yoko A008R's.

My wife has P600's on her VW GTI 16V.  I autocrossed it once this summer, and
found the P600's to also be very predictable.  (Given the car's weight
distribution, I couldn't get the fronts to stop rolling, even with 50 lb.!  The
rears didn't spend much time on the ground!)  The tires' squeals were evenly
matched with the driver's.

People have said nice things about the Yoko AVS series for "all-season" type
applications.  You can get 'em in dry, intermediate, and wet designs; most folks
go for the intermediates, I think.

Kevin

Now this is all my opinion:

Several people I have talked to seem to think that it is not worth the
cost to change from a 944 to a 944s.  Sure there is more HP, but not
enough more to justify the expense.  Even if you do so, also figure in
the cost of a new computer box or at the very least chips (help here
netters).

I would think that it would be better to upgrade the entire engine
rather than just the head as the belt-system is significantly different.

The early 944s (to 85.5) have the early suspension.  Although all 944s
have the same brakes, the early cars have the 924 stamped steel
suspension.  The front A-arms are prone to fatigue.

Concider another alternative:  selling the 944 and picking up a turbo.
You will almost double horsepower, get an improved suspension, nicer
interior (than an early car) with climate control, killer brakes,
sturdier transmission, better aerodynamics, etc.

Improving to the 944S would only be a wise choice if the parts are
available at bargain prices.

These are only my opinions, which are usually wrong anyway.

Steve

--

Subject: Re: 944 Upgrade to 944S
Message-Id: <90901215225309/0004056890NB3EM@mcimail.com>

It will be cheaper than buying a 944S, but it won't be cheaper than selling your
944 and buying a (used, of course) 944S.  The top end of the engine is
completely different, pistons on up.  The clutch is different, as is the
transmission.  It has the same gear ratios as the 951 (944 turbo), but the same
final as the 944.  Different fuel rail, different manifold, different air
induction system (for example, look at the filter and its housing).

The DME is quite different.  If my facts and inferences are correct, the 944S
was the first 944-series car to use Bosch's ML.3 version of Motronic, which
includes adaptive logic (ie, it learns from experience and forgets when you
unhook the battery), sequential injection, two knock sensors (944 has none, 951
has one), charcoal cannister purge control, and during the course of MY '87, a
diagnostic port.  The last item allows a tester (tool no. 9268) to be hooked up,
and various stored pieces of information to be displayed.

And you don't even want to ask what those little "16 Ventiler" trim pieces cost!

Kevin

Follow on to my first posting:

This was the only relevent reply so far (yes, I know the shift throw is long,
I am used to driving a stick, etc) and it only confirms that this condition
exists in other cars.

I have some possible reasons for the condition that I have heard from other
911 drivers.  I was hoping to get confirmation of some of these from others
on this mailing list.

Here are some of the possibilities, does anyone out there have any more info?

1)      Clutch hot spot.  Clutch is not disengaging completly.  The person
that suggested this also asked if there was a slight vibration of the 
shift while going into third (there is).

1a)     My own corellary to (1), clutch cable needs lubrication/replacement.

2)      Bushings in shift linkage need replacement.  These are located 
under the access cover in the "drive shaft hump" ;-) between the rear 
seats.  Mine appear tight.  Does anyone know if when these wear out if
it is hard or easy to determine?  I could not see any play when I wiggled
the shift linkage, but I would not consider what I did to be a definative 
test.

3)      Other shift/transmission problems, i.e., syncros, whatever.

I would appreciate any experience anyone has with items 1 through 2 above,
as this is where I suspect the problem is.

A related piece of information is that unless the clutch is ON THE FLOOR
when coming out of first, the transmission tends to "pop" a little as
first is disengaged.  I road tested 6 or 8 cars before buying mine.  I 
think that every one did this, execpt (maybe) the one that had just had
the transmission rebuilt.  Oh yeah, the clutch engagment point seems
to be reasonably high (off the floor), i.e. I think that the clutch is 
adjusted correctly.  This leads me to suspect item 1a) above.  Any hints
on clutch cable servicing/replacement?

Comments?

On the tire questions, thanks for everyones suggestions.  If anyone else out 
there who has driven on the comp TA's would like to send me their impressions,
I would appreciate it, as these look like what I will be buying.

One more tire point, Price club has the Yokohama A403 (225/50/16) for about 
$130.  The comp TA is about the same.  They do not stock 205/55/16 in any 
brand.  This is the Sunnyvale (CA) store.  Does anyone know of a bay area
price club or costco that stocks front tires?


Thanks, Soren.

To: laforce@krypton.arc.nasa.gov
Subject: Re:  New and improved 911 shift & tire questions.
Cc: porschephiles@bcm.tmc.edu


First an answer on the tire question: Do not buy the Comp TAs, if
you want to enjoy your car. I own a 78 SC and have put more than 50,000
miles on it in the past two years with four sets of tires.
Pirellis P7s:  mediocre tires, stick only reasonably well, don't last
               (13,000 miles), terrible in wet, feel good and fairly
                predictable.
Comp Ta:        terrible!!!! I just didn;t like them. First of all they
                are load. very, very load! at about 70 to 80 you get this
                very annoying tire noise, a bit like a truck. They are
                also not that good in wet and I had one additional problem:
                Comp TAs are bigger than other brands of the same size.
                With the same size tires my comp TAs rubbed at the fender
                lip, while the Pirellis and Yokahamas didn't! Lasted quite
                well, at 15,000 I was through to the belts. (rears only, 
                obviously).
Yokahama A008:  I really liked these and therefore now bought a second set
                of the same. They are very smooth, very quiet tires and
                are very, very sticky! My car handles much better than with
                the Comps or Pirellis. They are also very predictable, you
                know exactly when they are about to give. They are not bad
                in rain, but terrible in rain as they wear down. The first
                set lasted me  about 12,500 for the rears, not too long, but 
                worth every penny I spend.
                The handling with the Yokahamas is just fantastic, and comfort 
                is excellent.

On your transmission problems.

I rebuild mine 45,000 miles ago. I put new synchros for first and second and
now my second is very stiff too. I think I just got a very stiff synchro.
Some rings were easy to install, but I remember the second gear one was
almost impossible to get in. Another thing we may have to do is machine the
transmission case and put inserts in as guides for the shift rods. Mine had
a little play, not enough to justify getting the housing machined at the time.
Now I wonder whether that little play causes some of the stiffness.  One
piece of advice. When you pull apart your tranny, don't loosen the shift fork
bolts if you do not plan to adjust it. I have checked adjustment on a couple
of them, and it was right on, so, if you leave it at the factory setting, it
will probably be perfect upon reassembly.
Also, don't be scared taking a 915 tranny apart. It is actually quite easy
and after you have rebuild one, you really understand what is happening
inside with every shift. 

Karl

Received: by WILKINS.IAIMS.BCM.TMC.EDU (AA14934); Tue, 18 Dec 90 22:01:24 CST
Date: Tue, 18 Dec 90 22:01:24 CST
From: Stan Hanks <stanh@wilkins.iaims.bcm.tmc.edu>
Message-Id: <9012190401.AA14934@WILKINS.IAIMS.BCM.TMC.EDU>
To: merithew@jessica.stanford.edu, porschephiles@bcm.tmc.edu
Subject: Re:  914 oil cooler seals, jacking

I have replaced the seals with the engine in the car, and while you
*CAN* do it, you need to either have small hands and arms (I don't)
or access to someone who does. You basicly wind up removing part of the
engine shrouding, loosening the oil cooler, fishing the old seals out,
sliding the new seals in, and putting it all back together. It's almost
easier to do it by dropping the engine.

As to jacking the car, I had a custom piece of metal welded up that fits over
the inverted dimple things that are the *REAL* jack points of the car
that runs from the L to the R side dimples, with a support in the middle
for a Large Floor Jack. That way, I can jack up the whole rear or front of
the car at once, and support the bar with jack stands (which solves the old
now-that-I've-jacked-you-up-where-do-the-jack-stands-go problem). I'll see
if I can dig up the "engineering drawings" for that piece for posting here.

Stan

From porschephiles-request@bcm.tmc.edu  Wed Dec 19 08:55:06 1990

Received: from bcm.tmc.edu by WILKINS.IAIMS.BCM.TMC.EDU (AA15841); Wed, 19 Dec 90 08:49:38 CST

Message-Id: <9012191451.AA05669@bcm.tmc.edu>
From: fwk@homxc.att.com
Date: Wed, 19 Dec 90 09:28 EST
Original-From: homxc!fwk (F W Kerfoot, Iii +1 201 949 6348)
To: porschephiles@bcm.tmc.edu
Subject: jacking 914s Also, what's the best point to use to jack the car up from underneath? My Haynes says I can use the 'rear radius arms'.  Are they talking about the (narrow) metal bar that runs the width of the car just fore of the engine?  It looks like you would put some bizarre torques on important parts if you jacked the car from there...

which is the support for the front engine mount, frequently, without a 
problem. I believe that it is a widely accepted procedure. My car had the
cast type bar used in later series cars, but I believe it is accepted
practice with the earlier fabricated bar as well.

Although you didnt mention it, let me caution you against jacking the car via
the engine or transmission, as is possible in a 911. The 914 front engine
mounts, unlike the 911 mounts, load the rubber in tension when the
engine lifts the car. This tears the rubber. The engine doesnt drop (the
rubber is loaded in compression when the car is supporting the engine),
but there is no lateral support, and nothing to keep the front of the
engine from lifting on hard acceleration in low gears (due to axle
reaction torque).

For most utility jacking, I used a Sears floor jack. The 6" or so "cup"
which normally does the lifting is removable, and the portion of the
jack remaining is a nice fit on the jacking protuberances (or whatever
they are called) on the bottom of the car. If you are considering a floor
jack, you might want to look for one with this feature, or, if you have
one, you might also be lucky. By the way, removing the cup makes the jack
low enough to get under even very lowered 914s.

Frank Kerfoot
fwk@homxc.att.com

>On your transmission problems.

>I rebuild mine 45,000 miles ago. 

>Also, don't be scared taking a 915 tranny apart. It is actually quite easy
>and after you have rebuild one, you really understand what is happening
>inside with every shift.

I agree.  I broke a mainshaft on my 901 transmission a few years ago,
trying to make it through heavy traffic with a broken clutch cable...
The repair was far easier than I expected, although the mainshaft cost
me a few days wages...  The shift forks did not need to be readjusted,
so it was about a two hour job, not including r&r.

The differential is another matter.  I put a VW bug transmission in a
bus once, which required flopping the ring gear to the opposite side
(bus axles rotate opposite to bugs, due to reduction gearing at the
wheels).  I pulled the axles, cleaned the outside, and took the
transmission to Berkey Lee VW/Porsche, in Albany, and paid one hour's
labor to have a mechanic set it up for me.  It still howled.  Getting
the preload on the differential carrier bearings is a matter of selecting
the proper number of gaskets on each side.  It can be checked with
plastigauge, but its not quite so simple as replacing synchros or gear
sets.  I may have to do it soon, however - the 741 transmission in
my 356 leaks from the side covers, and tightening the bolts does not
solve the problem.  I don't think I can just count the gaskets and
reassemble with the same number of new gaskets, since they come in
different thicknesses.  Any comments?

-Dave Erickson

Subject: Leaking transmission side-covers

    Date: Wed, 19 Dec 90 11:29:31 PST

    leaks from the side covers, and tightening the bolts does not solve

Although not entirely applicable, the valve covers on 911 engines have
similar problems: since they distort slightly when the nuts are
tightened, there is a ever-so-slight bowing between mount points
eventually, with heat and age, this bow is set into the metal.
Tightening the bolts more exacerbates the problem, and the result is
warped valve covers.

This fact was brought close to home when I trued the mating surface of
my valve covers recently by lapping them on a flat surface:  the areas
around the mounting holes were much higher than the areas inbetween!  A
good bit of elbow grease and oil later, they are much better, and I have
a HOPE of having leak-free covers.

Generalize this idea at will; your milage may vary.


	- pz.

Subject: differential work (was: 911 transmissions..)

>>Also, don't be scared taking a 915 tranny apart. It is actually quite easy
>>and after you have rebuild one, you really understand what is happening
>>inside with every shift.
>
>I agree.  I broke a mainshaft on my 901 transmission a few years ago,

One more vote in the same direction. I have also used the trick of not
disturbing the adjustment of the shift forks on several 901 gearboxes,
with good results.

>solve the problem.  I don't think I can just count the gaskets and
>reassemble with the same number of new gaskets, since they come in
>different thicknesses.  Any comments?
>
>-Dave Erickson

In the past I have simply measured the thickness of the old gasket
with a micrometer. I would measure it several places (or equivalently
measure several of the pieces it will undoubtedly come off in).
Also make sure you measure all (but not more than all) of the composite
layers which were between the metal surfaces of interest.
Assuming you use a micrometer with the relatively standard .250" or so
faces and you use the micrometer's click stop (or friction stop),
I don't think the higher force due to the small measurement area
will give any significant error. Also measure the new gaskets before
you put them in. I did carrier bearings on a 901 box this way with
good results.

Frank Kerfoot
fwk@homxc.att.com

From: dave@kura.jpl.nasa.gov (Dave Skinner)
Message-Id: <9012262119.AA13902@kura.Jpl.Nasa.Gov>
To: porschephiles@bcm.tmc.edu
Subject: A Porsche 911 Fire

A 1986 Porsche 911 ended up sitting in some water up to the
bottom of its doors.  Apparently because it got wet, it would
not start.  It was towed home and placed in an attached garage
to dry out.  Sometime during the night, the car caught fire.
This destroyed the car and severely damaged the house.  The
Fire Department traced the fire back to the engine compartment
of the car.  What little we have learned since indicates that
the absence of "fused linkages" caused a short which led to
the fire.  I am trying to learn if this was a fluke or whether
this is a known problem with Porsche 911s.  If any of the
following 3 categories apply to you, please contact me personally
at the Internet e-mail address listed below:

(1) If you know of other such fires involving Porches

(2) If you are a sufficient export on Porsche 911s and cars in
    general to comment on how such wiring is usually implemented
    (say in American cars), or how such wiring should be
    implemented

(3) If you have any suggestions on how to widen the scope of this
    search

Actually any comments whatsoever would be appreciated.
Thanks.

Dave Skinner
dave@kirdu.jpl.nasa.gov (128.149.75.14)

here's a technical/political question for the ether..

howcumizzit nearly every manufacturer has their own, weird, solution
to the limited-slip problem.  you name it, someone does it.  fluid
couplings, clutch packs, visco-electric (the fluid changes viscosity
in the presence of an electric field), and of course there's porsche
with its electronically controlled diff locks.

the porsche systems use all sorts of inputs like side-loading, throttle
position, etc.

am i mistaken or is this all just to avoid paying royalties to the
company that owns the patents for the torsen/quaife solution, which
so far as i can tell is The Answer to torque distribution?

i'm as big a fan of electronic systems as the next guy, but jeez
i'm not sure i want somebody's program trying to "help" at the car's
limit by screwing with the differential.
